    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_361769821" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/361769821" />
    <a title="Jimmy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3357765-jimmy"><img alt="Jimmy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586258471p2/3357765.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/361769821?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jimmy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jimmy" href="/user/show/3357765-jimmy">Jimmy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3357765-jimmy?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer361769821" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18061344962018274990">Alastair Hannay writes that the "motivation" for Either/Or was "probably a combination of two things:" 1. breaking off with Regine Olsen and 2. his confrontation with Schelling's philosophy. I'm fascinated by the whole breakup story. I've long tried to figure out why he did it. I think it was Francis Bacon who said something like "He who has a wife and children has given hostages to fortune." Or maybe it was Kevin Bacon. In any case, I think Soren made the choice to write and he did not want any</span>
  <span id="freeText18061344962018274990" style="display:none">Alastair Hannay writes that the "motivation" for Either/Or was "probably a combination of two things:" 1. breaking off with Regine Olsen and 2. his confrontation with Schelling's philosophy. I'm fascinated by the whole breakup story. I've long tried to figure out why he did it. I think it was Francis Bacon who said something like "He who has a wife and children has given hostages to fortune." Or maybe it was Kevin Bacon. In any case, I think Soren made the choice to write and he did not want anything to stand in his way. Even his love for this young woman. <br /><br />As a poet myself, I was fascinated by the story in the first paragraph of Diapsalmata. He compares poets to the "unfortunates" who are being "slowly tortured by a gentle fire in Phalaris's bull." To the tyrant, "they sounded like sweet music." So with the poet. He needs to suffer to write. The words may be beautiful, but they are a product of suffering.<br /><br />I love the lines where Kierkegaard repeats "I can't be bothered" about opposite things. So he can't be bothered to ride and he can't be bothered to walk. He makes the point elsewhere as well that no matter what we do we will regret it. So there is no solution here on earth except suffering. He calls sorrow his life's "castle." The way others refer to their homes.<br /><br />I also love his famous image about the tile falling from a roof and hitting someone on the head. What's the point in making plans when you could walk out the door and get hit on the head by a falling tile and die. <br /><br />I think these lines are the beginning of existentialism:<br />"No one comes back from the dead, no one has entered the world without crying; no one is asked when he wishes to enter life, nor when he wishes to leave." <br /><br />Kierkegaard speaks of winning an essay contest at age 15 about the proof of the existence of the soul. Now at age 25, he cannot think of a single proof of the soul. This will all lead to the leap of faith. Forget proof, just believe. <br /><br />He makes his main point about tragedy in a bit of a contradiction: "there is an essential difference between ancient and modern tragedy" BUT "the concept of the tragic remains essentially unchanged." His first point is that the aesthetics of Aristotle still apply. And I have to agree with the genius of Aristotle. The difference with the modern concept of tragedy is that now the "hero stands and falls entirely on his own deeds." In the past it was more the sins of the father in the case of Antigone, or perhaps some excessive hubris as in the case of Oedipus. <br /><br />We are presented with three Shadowgraphs: Marie Beaumarchais from Goethe's Clavigo, Donna Elvira from Mozart's Don Giovanni, and Margrete from Goethe's Faust. All of them women who were mistreated by men. I can't help but go back again to Kierkegaard's personal life with Regine Olsen. More and more I believe he thought he was his own Don Juan. There may have been many reasons why he dumped her, such as the desire to focus on writing, but I think the whole Don Giovanni metaphor influenced him also. <br /><br />Kierkegaard's description of "The Unhappiest One":<br />âHe cannot become old, for he has never been young; he cannot become young, for he is already old. In one sense of the word he cannot die, for he is already old. In one sense of the word he cannot die, for he has not really lived; in another sense he cannot live, for he is already died. He cannot love, for love is in the present, and he has no present, no future, and no past; and yet he has a sympathetic nature, and he hates the world only because he loves it. He has no passion, not because he is destitute of it, but because simultaneously he has the opposite passion. He has no time for anything, not because his time is take up with something else, but because he has no time at all. He is impotent, not because he has no energy, but because his own energy makes him impotent.â<br /><br />He starts with the idea in Crop Rotation that "all men are boring." He should know.<br /><br />"Boredom is the root of all evil." When kids are bored, they cause trouble and misbehave. Hire nursemaids that know how to entertain children. I have to give Kierkegaard credit for understanding youngsters.<br /><br />Boredom can be traced back to "the very beginning of the world." Bored gods created humans. Bored Adam got Eve. Bored Adam and Eve conceived children. Bored children increased population. "The peoples were bored en masse so they built the Tower of Babel. Now bored people create a "constitutional assembly."<br /><br />Limit yourself and become resourceful. A prisoner in solitary confinement becomes amused by a spider. <br /><br />For married couples to promise "eternal love" is absurd. They should set a date they could "perhaps keep to."<br /><br />When a man tires of his wife and throws her out, he is thought of as contemptible. Kierkegaard does not understand this.<br /><br />Having a wife limits your freedom and "travel boots." A wife and children eliminates them.<br /><br />Have the courage to break off a marriage he says. <br /><br />In The Seducer's Diary, he speaks about seduction as if it were an art form. He considers engagement as "a purely invention and reflects no credit at all on its inventor." And it has nothing to do with love. Engagement loses its sense of eroticism. The seducer carries on his seduction until just the right moment. It must not be too soon. And he continues to promote the idea of female virginity which has been a bane on women for so long. <br /><br />"Once resistance is gone, love is only weakness and habit." . . . "If I were a god, I would do what Neptune did for a nymph: change her into a man." <br /><br />Kierkegaard believes that if there is a future, there is an either/or choice. I have gradually lost that belief in my own life. In any moment, I feel the past weighing down on me like a freight train. It feels that I may have a choice about tomorrow, but when it arrives it is no different than this moment. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18061344962018274990" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_766181820" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/766181820" />
    <a title="Edward III" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23129272-edward-iii"><img alt="Edward III" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1376739005p2/23129272.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/766181820?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward III" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward III" href="/user/show/23129272-edward-iii">Edward III</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer766181820" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7661021607125573503">This book is one of those rare things, a philosophical work that might actually change you. Works of philosophy should either make you look at the world differently, or yourself differently. Either/Or is in the latter class.<br/><br/>This is the first book by the first existentialist philospher, the Dane Soren Kierkegaard. In many ways it is unusual, such as I have described above, and also in terms of its structure. It is written under a pseudonym (Victor Eremita) who himself has not actually "written" t</span>
  <span id="freeText7661021607125573503" style="display:none">This book is one of those rare things, a philosophical work that might actually change you. Works of philosophy should either make you look at the world differently, or yourself differently. Either/Or is in the latter class.<br /><br />This is the first book by the first existentialist philospher, the Dane Soren Kierkegaard. In many ways it is unusual, such as I have described above, and also in terms of its structure. It is written under a pseudonym (Victor Eremita) who himself has not actually "written" the book but compiled it. It consists of two sections, the first being a series of essays and aphorisms by an unnamed "aesthete" (read: hedonist) who lives for nothing other than his own pleasure, the second being a response in the form of a series of letters to the aesthete from his friend judge Wilhelm, who represents the ethical mode of life. According to Eremita, he found these two opposed works in an antique writing desk, and he carries them around in briefcases meant for dueling pistols, to symbolize their antagonistic nature.<br /><br />It's clear that Eremita, if not Kierkegaard himself, wants us to choose between these two different modes of life, though it may not initially be clear just how they are incompatible, if at all. You'll have to read the book to find out, in fact that very question gives rise to one of many great surprises in this excellent work. Kierkegaard himself insisted that we either read the whole thing or leave it aside entirely, and once you have read both sections you will understand why. The two parts necessarily complement each other and form a unified whole, which doesn't fully make sense independently. This is why it is so strange that the various sections have been published separately. It is quite easy to find a copy of just part II, less easy to find only a copy of part I, and I have even found the longest essay in part I (The Seducer's Diary) published by itself. This seems to miss the point of the work.<br /><br />As it is, Either/Or is a masterpiece. In the Penguin Classics edition one essay from part I has been omitted, but the edition suffers little for it. There are countless moments of great poetic beauty in both sections, some poignant moments, and even a couple of moments where the reader literally bursts out in to laughter. That is rare for any author and rarer still for a philosopher, but such is the power of Kierkegaard's prose.<br /><br />But for all of Kierkegaard's mastery of his craft, the message itself is what really counts, and I won't spoil that for you. I will merely say that this book has had a considerable impact on my life, coming at just the right time and saying just what I needed to hear. Either/Or was a kick in the pants for me, and this is really what any work of philosophy should be. I do not give out 5 star rankings for many books. This one ranks among the best I have read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7661021607125573503" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2955551843" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2955551843" />
    <a title="GastjÃ¤le" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58816143-gastj-le"><img alt="GastjÃ¤le" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471703156p2/58816143.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2955551843?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="GastjÃ¤le" class="user" itemprop="url" name="GastjÃ¤le" href="/user/show/58816143-gastj-le">GastjÃ¤le</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2955551843" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer836702846363542563">Kierkegaard loves puzzles, and here's one: what could one possibly glean out of a book edited by a pseudonym, comprising two sets of more-or-less thematically united letters, the first set mostly written by another pseudonym (who also presents a diary written yet by another pseudonym) and the other set written by yet another pseudonym who replies directly to the writer of the first set, basing their arguments on the lifestyle and personality of that pseudonym (which the reader doesn't really hav</span>
  <span id="freeText836702846363542563" style="display:none">Kierkegaard loves puzzles, and here's one: what could one possibly glean out of a book edited by a pseudonym, comprising two sets of more-or-less thematically united letters, the first set mostly written by another pseudonym (who also presents a diary written yet by another pseudonym) and the other set written by yet another pseudonym who replies directly to the writer of the first set, basing their arguments on the lifestyle and personality of that pseudonym (which the reader doesn't really have much information of)? To further complicate things, the letters are very dense indeed, often taking shelter under Hegelian verbiage and irony, sometimes professing ideas which aren't strictly attributed to the pseudonym. And to turn on the Bloody Hard Mode Indeed, the initiated Kierkegaard reader is well aware that the melancholy trickster of Copenhagen did not ascribe to either view of his either/or. <br /><br />Obviously, this rules out the possibility of reading into the text and attempting to figure out what the real Kierkegaard thought. One could come up with all kinds of scenarios of A being the young Kierkegaard before and during the infamous "earthquake", with the B being a shadow of what Kierkegaard was actually striving to be, yet would never want to stop at. What one might adduce for this scenario is that both viewpoints are extremely suggestively written, with plenty of viewpoints and counter-answers to hypothetical questions, so it obviously must be something that Kierkegaard has felt to be pretty close to him. Unfortunately, that would border on presumption. However, it is also equally obvious that it would be playing the aesthete if one were to read <i>Either/Or</i> as a mere piece of fiction: it makes constant references to the age it's living in, accuses it of all kinds of moral turpitude, waxes lyrical on matters of beauty, discusses matters of obvious ethical import in a non-artistic way and sets itself (rather facetiously) in the canon of Western philosophy.<br /><br />Thereby one is given the following opportunities: to examine the ideas propounded by both pseudonyms (hereafter A for the aesthetic and B for the ethical) or to examine the characters of A and B. And here the disjunction need not be exclusive.<br /><br />Speaking of things on the surface level, both A and B are very suggestive and clever in their argumentation. Both employ poetic devices, defer to authorities and anticipate arguments, which they then proceed to rebut. They are both also very much engrossed in difficult terminology, which they do not necessarily define understandably, thus creating a shroud of impenetrable fog around their theses. The main argumentative/rhetorical difference, however, lies in their respective audiences: A addresses both everyone and the mysterious Symparanekromenoi, the fellowship of buried lives, whereas B directs his disquisitions at A. Hereby A gains the advantage of the universal and B that of hindsight and getting the last word. <br /><br />Initially, I was rather led by the Byronesque charm of A and afterwards thought him to be fairly rotten because of B's pathological analysis of him. But upon cogitating on them both, I came to the conclusion that things were not so simple. First of all, simply labelling A as the aesthetic and B as the ethical is not satisfactory, if we are to adhere to the terminology A and B are using. B said that the aesthetic implies that you live in immediacy, whereas the ethical means that you become what you become (which I take to mean inner development after choosing yourself instead of some shady relativity of persona). Now, I don't presume to truly know what Kierkegaard might mean by "immediacy", but I can only assume that it might have something to do with seeing oneself as sufficient and projecting everything through oneself only, with little to no regard to seeing oneself as a universal being. B would love to see this immediacy as the existence of someone who does not assert their own persona and is thus lost in the throng, only pursuing aimless pleasures that are always contingent upon external stimuli. But this is not really what A professes them to be in my opinion: the whole point of aesthetic living seems to hinge on asserting oneself and one's tastes, and clinging to those with absolute rigour. Yes, the stimuli can be external, but it's the inner workings of the mind that count here first and foremost, for the aesthetic judgment is performed within the subject. Likewise, the ethical existence is equally contingent on external factors (such as the ethical code itself and the validation of the universal through empiricism), so there's no need to deride aesthetic living based on externality. Furthermore, what B says about the ethical person choosing oneself through repentance/despair and thus becoming the universal, is truly problematic: how does an experience of beauty differ in terms of the phenomenological subject as opposed to repentance/despair? How is the relation between the particular and the universal different in aesthetic judgment than it is in ethical living? And what does it really mean to be both the particular and the universal?<br /><br />What makes things even more complicated is the question whether A and B really are good representatives of their respective, purported manners of existence. Simply put, I'm truly confused. But herein lies the greatness of the book: the characters might not, indeed, be paradigmatic at all. They're ambiguous instances, who are labelled only by others who assume too much. And it is only by dint of assumption that I could label them myself: <br /><br />- A is a chap who sees regret as an ultimate evil and thus forbears from aiming at any lofty aspirations, preferring to indulge in what life can offer immediately instead of seeking constant edification and universally-minded development. He shuns at the diabolical pursuits of Johannes the Seducer, and thus implies that causing damage to others is not the way to live. He however does have some degree of respect towards artistic and determined seducers like the foregoing fellow and Don Juan, yet the beauty of it all comes to A not from the conquests but from the lack of regret. Overall, A favours living in the pure, beautiful moment and thus to minimise pointless regrets of the past and of the future. The universal means little to A personally, but it does, from time to time, enhance his self-veneration.<br /><br />- B is a chap who finds the value of life in the interplay between the particular and the universal. He applauds the commonplace, for therein he seems real beauty and stability: everyone finding their own place in the world and fulfilling their own duties as themselves. The fine thing about life is to discover oneself as an ethical being and thus to see oneself from the perspective of the universal. What these ethics are, is however left unsaid: this probably signifies that the form of ethical living is what matters, just like how it's the form of the Kantian imperative which marks the true ethical. <br /><br />Put this way, both A and B definitely have their points. The demerits of their respective lifestyles comes to light more or less only when they try to justify them at the expense of the other. A comes up with all kinds of specious arguments about having unbeginning as his principles, whereas B forgets his own relation to things while pontificating about the subjective dimension of ethical living. What's especially ironic about B is that he claims not to preach yet after providing copious evidence to the contrary, he finally appends a prayer-cum-sermon at the end of his letter, the tenor of which is to show that "against God we are always in the wrong". To me, it shows that B is using such an ultimate stance as ammunition against A, rather than resigning himself to the same truth. Now <i>this</i> kind of hypocrisy is just the kind of thing I would assume Kierkegaard criticised about the ethical mode of existence. Whereas, when it comes to the aesthetic one, I presume he meant to show us that there is barely any relation to the infinite, i.e. God - an obvious shortcoming for anyone in Kierkegaard's opinion. <br /><br />That is, however, my own, personal analysis and it may be utterly in the wrong. It could be that Kierkegaard was simply a confused writer. But I don't want to posit such a possibility: I don't think this book is painstakingly calculated, yet I believe that most of the things are here for a reason. And since Kierkegaard was obviously on the alert when he walked in public, and since he clearly had an eye for observing the ways of the society, the contradictory elements seem to sit very comfortably in this book. In other words, this is a book that one can safely call multilayered. <br /><br />But besides the juxtaposition and the character analysis, what else did gain out of <i>Either/Or</i>? Nothing or everything? Something. <br /><br />Kierkegaard's finesse as an author lies in his paradoxical way of discussing things. By paradoxical, I mean that his style is both fragmentary and perseveringly unified. He jumps from one viewpoint to another and digresses with dubious Ã©lan, yet he also hammers home the points he wishes to make. He bids the reader to enter a maze, and somehow he always emerges from it unscathed. (By "he", I'm obviously referring to Kierkegaard â God rest my weary, thorn-ravished body, mind and soul.) He almost forces your mind to wander, leaving you to ponder on individual points and making up your mind about them. He also frustrates you, which could mean anything, but for me it causes me to push him aside for a while and attacking the points from my own perspective â just what philosophy should be about! Yet one must also admit that he is a stunningly good writer when he wants to: the translator, Alastair Hannay, made sure to underscore this with a superbly crafted translation full of beautiful words and images. <br /><br />Yet he indeed does frustrate one. He frustrates one a lot. Most of the chapters here are long and at times tedious accounts of all sorts of things, and even the beautiful writing will lose its lustre when the long sentences simply keep on coming unremittingly. And that in turn contributed to my wandering attention, which might be the cause of the overall confusion. Perhaps I've horribly misjudged the characters in this treatise! Perhaps my attention simply kept on wandering and I ended up spinning a threadbare analytical web out of miscellaneous observations, instead of weaving a beautiful canvass of patience and perspicuity. With Kierkegaard, one simply has to accept such possibilities and live with the incurred anxiety. And if Truth comes crashing through the window, pointing its finger of judgment at one, laughing with utter contempt, then it simply behoves one to: Despair! </span>
  <a data-text-id="836702846363542563" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Eyu9tiqSyUzITHZI4gzE5BV5g/Bqw5UvuCmO8SDn+2G70+0rrMrxQFOEPVQbSobmC8j0/RKy6N2Zn0SwOrZ+Qw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('KImFKSeYzuLJIyimDJC+yOyaCvl4caMcojN0BCrhiXaAcdW0ocD27lLrY7r11vzK8it99AAA3u6Dhb5FMLAMVA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_19506'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19506.The_World_as_Will_and_Representation_Vol_1?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The World as Will and Representation, Vol. 1<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/11682.Arthur_Schopenhauer?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Arthur Schopenhauer<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.19 avg rating &mdash; 8,422 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1818\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer8006788426979368310\">Arthur Schopenhauer\'s Die Welt als Wille und Vorstellung is one of the most important philosophical works of the nineteenth century, the basic statement of one important stream of post-Kantian thought. It is without question Schopenhauer\'s greatest w<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8006788426979368310\" style=\"display:none\">Arthur Schopenhauer\'s Die Welt als Wille und Vorstellung is one of the most important philosophical works of the nineteenth century, the basic statement of one important stream of post-Kantian thought. It is without question Schopenhauer\'s greatest work. Conceived and published before the philosopher was 30 and expanded 25 years later, it is the summation of a lifetime of thought.\n\nFor 70 years, the only unabridged English translation of this work was the Haldane-Kemp collaboration. In 1958, a new translation by E. F. J. Payne appeared that decisively supplanted the older one. Payne\'s translation is superior because it corrects nearly 1,000 errors and omissions in the Haldane-Kemp translation, and it is based on the definitive 1937 German edition of Schopenhauer\'s work prepared by Dr. Arthur HÃ¼bscher. Payne\'s edition is the first to translate into English the text\'s many quotations in half a dozen languages. Nietzsche rewrites the former as a history of cruelty, exposing the central values of the Judaeo-Christian and liberal traditions - compassion, equality, justice - as the product of a brutal process of conditioning designed to domesticate the animal vitality of earlier cultures. The result is a book which raises profoundly disquieting issues about the violence of both ethics and interpretation. Nietzsche questions moral certainties by showing that religion and science have no claim to absolute truth, before turning on his own arguments in order to call their very presuppositions into question. The Genealogy is the most sustained of Nietzsche\'s later works and offers one of the fullest expressions of his characteristic concerns. This edition places his ideas within the cultural context of his own time and stresses the relevance of his work for a contemporary audience.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"18198514536214572019\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/6172.S_ren_Kierkegaard">About SÃ¸ren Kierkegaard</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/6172.S_ren_Kierkegaard"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1202410387p3/6172.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/6172.S_ren_Kierkegaard">SÃ¸ren Kierkegaard
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,839
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4118385623786401074">SÃ¸ren Aabye Kierkegaard was a prolific 19th century Danish philosopher and theologian. Kierkegaard strongly criticised both the Hegelianism of his time and what he saw as the empty formalities of the Church of Denmark. Much of his work deals with religious themes such as faith in God, the institution of the Christian Church, Christian ethics and theology, and the emotions and feelings of individua</span>
  <span id="freeText4118385623786401074" style="display:none">SÃ¸ren Aabye Kierkegaard was a prolific 19th century Danish philosopher and theologian. Kierkegaard strongly criticised both the Hegelianism of his time and what he saw as the empty formalities of the Church of Denmark. Much of his work deals with religious themes such as faith in God, the institution of the Christian Church, Christian ethics and theology, and the emotions and feelings of individuals when faced with life choices. His early work was written under various pseudonyms who present their own distinctive viewpoints in a complex dialogue.<br /><br />Kierkegaard left the task of discovering the meaning of his works to the reader, because "the task must be made difficult, for only the difficult inspires the noble-hearted".Scholars have interpreted Kierkegaard variously as an existentialist, neo-orthodoxist, postmodernist, humanist, and individualist.<br /><br />Crossing the boundaries of philosophy, theology, psychology, and literature, he is an influential figure in contemporary thought.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4118385623786401074" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
